Liability for Releasing a Hunted Animal from a Muhrim's Hand
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for releasing a hunted animal from a muhrim's hand.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for releasing a hunted animal from a muhrim's hand, identify the proofs for each position, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
A muhrim's ownership of a lawfully hunted animal persists after ihram; releasing it incurs liability according to Abu Hanifa.

When a non-muhrim catches a game animal and then enters ihram, the animal remains in his possession. If another person releases it, is he liable? Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ on this point. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and the practical consequence of the dispute.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the dispute
Identify the key elements: a non-muhrim catches a game animal, then enters ihram. Another person releases the animal from his hand. The dispute: Is the releasee liable for compensation?
The animal is lawfully owned before ihram.
2
Identify the conflicting positions
Abu Hanifa holds the releasee is liable. The Sahibayn hold he is not liable. Note the basis for each.
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn argue the releasee is commanding good and forbidding evil, since the muhrim is prohibited from keeping the animal.
This is a principle of al-amr bi al-ma'ruf.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof and rebuttal
Abu Hanifa argues the muhrim owned the animal with respected ownership (milk muhtaram); ihram does not nullify that right. The releasee destroyed that right, so he is liable. The muhrim could have simply left the animal in a house; releasing it is transgression.
Abu Hanifa distinguishes the case where the animal was taken while already in ihram.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
The practical difference: According to Abu Hanifa, the releasee must pay the animal's value; according to the Sahibayn, he does not. Later Hanafi practice may prefer the Sahibayn's view by istihsan.
Not explicitly resolved in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أرسل صيدا في يدِ محرم إن أخذَهُ حلالاً ضَمِن، وإلا فلا. قوله : ومن أرسل ... الخ؛ قال في «الهداية»: فإن أصاب حلال صيداً ثم أحرم ثم أرسله من يده غيره، يضمن عند أبي حنيفة له، وقالا: لا يضمن؛ لأنَّ المرسل آمر بالمعروف ناه عن المنكر. وله: أنه ملك الصيد بالأخذ ملكاً مُحترماً، فلا يبطل احترامه بإحرامه، وقد أتلفه المرسل فيضمنه، بخلاف ما إذا أخذه في حالة الإحرام؛ لأنه لم يملكه، والواجب عليه ترك التعرّض، ويمكنه ذلك بأن يخلّيه في بيت، فإذا قطع يده كان متعدياً فيضمنه.
And whoever sends a hunted animal that is in the hand of a muhrim—if a non-muhrim takes it, he is liable, otherwise not. Commentary: He said in al-Hidayah: If a non-muhrim catches a game animal, then enters ihram, and then another person sends it from his hand, he is liable according to Abu Hanifa, but the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say he is not liable because the sender is commanding good and forbidding evil. And for him (Abu Hanifa) is that he owned the game by taking it as a respected ownership, so its respect is not nullified by his ihram, and the sender has destroyed it, so he is liable, unlike the case where he took it while in ihram, because he did not own it, and the obligation upon him is to avoid it, and he can do so by leaving it in a house, so if he cuts off its hand (i.e., releases it) he is a transgressor and is liable.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. The scenario: a non-muhrim catches a game animal, then enters ihram. Then another person releases the animal from his hand. Abu Hanifa holds the releasee liable because the muhrim had owned the animal through lawful capture, and ownership is respected despite ihram. The releasee caused loss. The Sahibayn say no liability because the releasee is ordering good (removing the prohibited animal). Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the muhrim could have simply left the animal in a house, so the releasee is a transgressor. The case is contrasted with taking the animal while in ihram, where no ownership is established.

Key Terms
مُحْرِم (muḥrim): A person who has entered the state of consecration (ihram) for Hajj or Umrah.
ضَمِن (ḍamina): He is liable; must pay compensation (damān) for the value of the animal.
صَيْد (ṣayd): Game animal (hunted or hunted animal) that is permissible to eat when not in ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the Sahibayn's view that the releasee is not liable?
A) The muhrim waived his right.
B) The releasee is commanding good and forbidding evil.
C) The animal was never owned by the muhrim.
D) The releasee is also a muhrim.

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute, Abu Hanifa holds the releasee liable for compensation because the muhrim's ownership right is not nullified by ihram, while the Sahibayn absolve him based on the principle of commanding good and forbidding evil.

One Muhrim Takes and Another Kills: Dual Compensation
Ruling on dual liability when two muhrims are involved with one animal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when two muhrims are involved with one game animal (one takes, one kills) and explain the recovery mechanism.

Core Idea
Both taker and killer owe full jaza'; the taker recovers from the killer because his liability was contingent.

In ihram, hunting is prohibited. But what happens when two muhrims are involved with the same animal? This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah clarifies a subtle scenario: one muhrim takes an animal (while not in ihram, as explained) and another kills it. The ruling determines liability and recovery.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the scenario
Identify the two muhrims and their actions: one takes (acquires) a game animal, the other kills it. Note that the taker is a muhrim but at the time of taking he was not in ihram (as per commentary).
The taker’s act of taking is still an infringement because he is a muhrim, even if not currently in ihram.
2
State the liability
Both owe a full compensation (jaza') – the taker because of his taking, the killer because of his killing.
The compensation is wajib for each, not shared.
3
Explain the recovery
The taker can recover from the killer the compensation he paid.
This is because the taker’s liability was on the verge of being dropped; he could have released the animal. The killer’s act made it definite.
4
Understand the rationale
The taker’s liability was contingent – he could have avoided it by releasing the animal. The killer’s act sealed the liability, so the ultimate responsibility falls on the killer.
This ensures justice: the taker is not out-of-pocket for an act that was not final.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قتل محرم صيد مثله، فكل يجزئ جزاءً كاملاً، ورَجَعَ آخِذُهُ على قاتله. قوله: أخذه؛ أي أخذ المحرم الآخر ذلك الصيد حال كونه غير محرم. قوله: فكل يجزئ؛ أي كل من المحرم الآخذ والمحرم القاتل يؤدّي جزاء الصيد، أما الآخذ فبأخذه، وأما القاتل فبقتله ورجع الآخذ على القاتل بما وجب عليه أداؤه: أي ضمنه ما أداه؛ لأنّ أخذه وإن كان موجباً للضمان، لكنه كان على شرف السقوط، فإنّه كان يمكنه أن يخلّيه، وإنّما تقرر عليه الجزاء بقتل القاتل.
If a muhrim kills a similar game animal, each (the taker and the killer) pays a full compensation, and the taker recovers from the killer. Commentary: 'His taking' means the other muhrim took that animal while being not in ihram. 'Each pays' means both the muhrim who took and the muhrim who killed pay the compensation for the animal—the taker because of his taking and the killer because of his killing; and the taker recovers from the killer what he had to pay, i.e., he is indemnified for what he paid because his taking, though it necessitated liability, was on the verge of being dropped since he could have released it, but the compensation became established due to the killing by the killer.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This passage gives a ruling where two muhrim are involved with the same animal: one takes it (while not in ihram, as clarified by the commentary), and the other kills it. Both must pay a full compensation (jaza') for the animal—the taker because he took it (which is prohibited for a muhrim), and the killer because he killed it. However, the taker can later recover the amount from the killer, because the taker's liability was not definite; he could have simply released the animal. The killer's act made the compensation binding.

Key Terms
جَزاء (jazā'): The required compensation for killing game while in ihram; it may be slaughtering a similar animal, feeding the poor, or fasting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the compensation paid by the taker when the animal is killed by another muhrim?
A) The taker pays nothing because the killer is solely responsible.
B) The taker pays full compensation and recovers it from the killer.
C) The taker and killer split the compensation equally.
D) The taker pays half and the killer pays half.

Key Takeaway
When one muhrim takes and another kills the same animal, both pay full compensation, but the taker recovers from the killer because the taker's liability was contingent.

Blood Penalty for Mufrid and Qarin
Definition and expansion of the blood penalty rule for mufrid and qarin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the blood penalty obligations of a mufrid and a qarin, and apply the exception for crossing the miqat without ihram.

Core Idea
A qarin owes two dams for each prohibited act of ihram, unless the act cannot be doubled.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary clarifies how blood penalties (dam) are multiplied for a qarin (one performing Hajj and Umrah together) compared to a mufrid (Hajj only). The rule is straightforward: every prohibited act requiring one dam for a mufrid requires two for a qarin, because the violation affects both rites. However, an important exception exists when the act is not doubled, such as crossing the miqat without ihram and then entering as a qarin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of pilgrims
Recognize that a mufrid performs only Hajj, while a qarin combines Hajj and Umrah in one ihram. This difference is key to the multiplication of penalties.
Qiran is a type of Hajj in the Hanafi school.
2
State the basic rule of multiplication
For each prohibited act of ihram (like wearing stitched clothes, applying perfume), a mufrid owes one dam; a qarin owes two dams.
The rule applies only to acts prohibited due to ihram itself, not to obligations or Haram-related acts.
3
Understand the scope of the prohibition
Excluded from the multiplication are wajibat (e.g., sa'y, ramy) and acts related to the Haram (e.g., cutting grass). These incur a single penalty even for a qarin.
4
Recognize the exception: crossing the miqat without ihram
If a pilgrim passes the miqat without ihram and then enters as a qarin, only one dam is due, because the offense (crossing without ihram) is single and not doubled for each rite.
If, however, the pilgrim enters ihram for Hajj first, then later enters ihram for Umrah without returning to the hill, two separate dams are owed.
5
Apply the rule to a case
Given a specific scenario, determine whether the act is a prohibition of ihram, and whether it is doubled. Conclude the number of dams due.
Use the distinction between mufrid and qarin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما به دم. قوله: وما به... الخ؛ يعني كل فعل يجب به دم واحد على المفرد بالحج، فعلى القارن به إن فعله دمان، والمراد به فعل ما هو من محظورات الإحرام: أي ما حرم بسبب نفس الإحرام إلا من حيث كونه حجاً أو عمرة، ولا ما حرم عليه بسبب غير الإحرام، وذلك كاللبس والتطيب، فخرج ما لو ترك واجباً كالسعي والرمي، فإنّ فيه كفارة واحدة على القارن أيضاً، وخرج أيضاً ما لو قطع حشيش الحرم، فلا يتعدد الجزاء به. وزيادة التفصيل في هذا البحث في «البحر الرائق». ثم المتن: على المفرد فعلى القارن دمان إلا بجواز الوقتِ غير محرم. قوله: على المفرد؛ وكذا الصدقة تتعدّد على القارن فيما تجب فيه. قوله: إلا بجواز... الخ؛ يعني إذا تجاوز الميقات ولم يحرم، ثم أحرم قارناً لا يجب عليه إلا دم واحد؛ لعدم تعدد الجناية، نعم لو جاوز فأحرم بحج ثم دخل مكة فأحرم بعمرة ولم يعد إلى الحل يلزمُهُ دمان دمّ للمجاوزة، ودم لتركه ميقات العمرة. كذا في «النهاية». قوله: لأن الواجب؛ علة لما فهم من الاستثناء من عدم تعدّد الدم.
And what incurs a blood penalty. Commentary: It means every act that requires one dam for the mufrid (individual Hajj performer) requires two for the qarin (combined Hajj and Umrah performer), provided it is an act of the prohibitions of ihram—i.e., what is forbidden due to ihram itself, not due to being Hajj or Umrah, nor what is forbidden due to something else like wearing sewn clothes and perfume. Thus excluded is what he leaves of obligations like sa'y and ramy, for which there is one expiation for the qarin as well; and also excluded is cutting grass of the Haram, for which the penalty does not multiply. Further detail is in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. Then the matn: For the mufrid, one dam; for the qarin, two dams, except when crossing the miqat without being in ihram. Commentary: 'For the mufrid' means similarly the charity is multiplied for the qarin where it is required. 'Except when crossing' means if he passes the miqat without ihram and then enters as a qarin, only one dam is due because there is no multiplicity of offense. However, if he passes and enters ihram for Hajj, then enters Mecca and enters ihram for Umrah without returning to the hill, he owes two dams: one for crossing without ihram and one for leaving the miqat of Umrah. This is in al-Nihayah. 'Because the obligation' is the reason for understanding from the exception that there is no multiplicity of blood.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This passage defines the blood penalty (dam) in Hajj. A mufrid (performing only Hajj) owes one dam for each prohibited act of ihram, while a qarin (combining Hajj and Umrah in one ihram) owes two dams for the same act, because the act violates both rites. However, the rule applies only to acts prohibited due to ihram itself (like wearing stitched clothes, applying perfume), not to obligations (like sa'y) or acts related to the Haram. An exception: when a person crosses the miqat without ihram and then enters as a qarin, only one dam is due because the offense (crossing without ihram) is single. But if he enters ihram for Hajj first and later for Umrah without returning to the hill, he owes two separate dams.

Key Terms
دَم (dam): A blood penalty: slaughtering a sheep as expiation for violating a prohibition of ihram.
مُفْرِد (mufrid): One who performs only Hajj (not combined with Umrah) in a single ihram.
قَارِن (qārin): One who performs Hajj and Umrah together with a single ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many blood penalties (dam) does a qarin owe for wearing sewn clothes while in ihram?
A) One dam
B) Two dams
C) None
D) One dam but with an alternative

Key Takeaway
A qarin owes two dams for each prohibited act of ihram, except when the act is a single offense (like crossing miqat without ihram), in which case only one dam is due.

Two Muhrim Killing One Animal: Each Must Pay Full Jaza'
Ruling that two muhrim each owe full compensation for killing one animal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two muhrim kill one game animal and explain why each owes full compensation.

Core Idea
Each muhrim is individually liable for killing in ihram, even if the animal is one.

This passage addresses a scenario: two people in ihram jointly kill a single game animal. The ruling is clear: each must pay a full compensation (jaza'). The reason is that the prohibition against hunting in ihram applies to each individual, and the act of killing is attributed to each. This rule distinguishes Hanafi from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Find the scenario: two muhrim (people in ihram) kill one animal. The ruling: each owes a separate jaza' (compensation).
The passage uses 'yuthna' (doubled) to emphasize the duplication.
2
Understand the legal basis
The obligation arises from the act of killing in ihram, which is attached to each individual muhrim. The unity of the victim does not reduce multiple liabilities.
Hanafi principle: the cause (sabab) is the prohibited act, not the result.
3
Compare with other schools
The Shafi'i school holds that one compensation suffices collectively. Note the difference in underlying reasoning.
This comparison highlights the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).
4
Apply to a modern scenario
Suppose two pilgrims in ihram accidentally kill a gazelle. According to Hanafi, each must offer a sacrificial animal. This ensures each fulfills their obligation.
Application reinforces the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُثنى جزاء صيد قتله محرمان. قوله: ويُثني... الخ؛ مجهول من التثنية، يعني إذا قتل اثنان محرمان صيداً واحداً يجب على كل واحد منهما جزاؤه على حدة؛ لأنه وجب بسبب القتل في الإحرام وهو متعدّد، وإن أتحد المقتول.
And the compensation of an animal killed by two muhrimen is doubled. Commentary: 'And is doubled' is passive from tathniyah, meaning if two muhrimen kill one animal, each one owes his compensation separately because it became obligatory due to killing in ihram, which is multiple, even though the killed animal is one.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage states the rule: when two individuals in ihram jointly kill a single game animal, each must pay a full jaza' (compensation) independently. The reason is that the obligation arises from the act of killing while in ihram, and since the killing involves two independent culprits, each incurs a separate penalty. The unity of the victim does not reduce the multiple obligations.

Key Terms
جَزاء (jazā'): The required penalty for killing game in ihram, which is a sacrificial animal or equivalent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two muhrimen jointly kill one game animal, how many compensations (jaza') are owed according to the Hanafi school?
A) One compensation shared equally.
B) Two compensations, one from each muhrim.
C) One compensation from the one who struck the fatal blow.
D) No compensation if they were unaware.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi, each muhrim bears full liability for killing game, so two muhrim mean two compensations.

Joint Compensation for One Animal Killed by Two Non-Muhrimen
Ruling with brief commentary on joint liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that two non-muhrimen owe one joint compensation for killing one game animal in the Haram and explain the rationale based on the sanctity of the Haram.

Core Idea
Compensation for Haram violations follows the animal, not the number of killers.

In the previous lesson, we saw that when a muhrim kills game, each muhrim owes a separate penalty. Here the situation changes: two people not in ihram kill a single animal in the Haram. Because the prohibition here stems from the sanctity of the Haram itself rather than the state of ihram, the penalty is linked to the animal. One animal, one compensation, shared equally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actors and the act
Note that the killers are non-muhrimen (not in ihram) and the animal is one. The act takes place within the Haram.
The Haram is the sacred precinct of Mecca where hunting is forbidden for everyone.
2
Recall the legal basis for compensation
Compensation (jaza') is due for violating the sanctity of the Haram by killing game. It is attached to the animal killed, not to the killers individually.
This contrasts with cases where killers are in ihram, where each owes a separate penalty.
3
Apply the principle to multiple killers
Since there is only one animal, the compensation is singular. Both killers share the responsibility equally (each owes half).
The joint liability arises because the penalty is a single obligation attached to the animal.
4
Distinguish from similar scenarios
If the killers were muhrimen, each would owe a full compensation because the penalty is for violating their own ihram, not merely for the animal.
This distinction is crucial for correct fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واتًحد لو قتل صيد الحرم حلالان. قوله: واتًحد... الخ؛ يعني إذا قتل اثنان غير محرمين صيدا واحدا من المحرم فعليهما جزاء واحد؛ لأنه جزاء المقتول وهو واحد.
And it is unified if two non-muhrimen kill a game animal of the Haram. Commentary: 'And it is unified' means if two non-muhrimen kill one animal from the prohibited area, they owe one compensation because it is the penalty for the killed animal, which is one.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage contrasts with the previous rule. When two non-muhrimen (people not in ihram) kill a single game animal within the Haram (the sacred precinct of Mecca), they owe only one joint compensation. The reason is that the compensation here is for violating the sanctity of the Haram by killing the animal, and since the animal is one, a single penalty suffices, regardless of the number of killers. This differs from the case where the killers are in ihram, where each incurs a separate penalty.

Key Terms
حَرَم (ḥaram): The sacred sanctuary of Mecca where hunting is prohibited even for non-muhrimen.
جزاء (jazā'): Compensation or penalty for illegally killing game in the Haram or by a muhrim.
مُحْرِم (muḥrim): A person in the state of ihram (during Hajj or Umrah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two non-muhrimen kill one game animal in the Haram. How many compensations are due?
A) Two separate compensations.
B) One compensation shared between them.
C) Each owes half a compensation (same as B).
D) No compensation because they are not in ihram.

Key Takeaway
When non-muhrimen kill game in the Haram, the penalty is one per animal, shared equally among all killers.

Muhrim Buying or Selling Game: Ruling and Nuances
Ruling with conditions and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a muhrim's transaction in game, distinguish between batil and fasid based on who hunted and who sold, and identify the underlying principle of mutaqawwim.

Core Idea
For a muhrim, game is not property — its sale/purchase is void if he hunted it in ihram.

This passage from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj clarifies the Hanafi ruling on a muhrim's transactions involving game. While the default ruling is that the transaction is void, the author distinguishes several scenarios based on who hunted the animal and who is the seller or buyer. These distinctions highlight the concept of mutaqawwim (legally valuable property) and its role in contract validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The main ruling states that if a muhrim sells or buys game that he personally hunted while in ihram, the transaction is batil (void).
The keyword is 'batala' – absolute nullity.
2
Note the condition for the core ruling
The void ruling is conditional on the muhrim being the hunter during his own ihram. If the hunting was done by a non-muhrim, the ruling changes.
The source says 'اصطاده حالة إحرامه' – hunted during his ihram.
3
Distinguish the two other scenarios
If the muhrim hunts and sells to a non-muhrim, the sale is valid (jai'z). If a non-muhrim hunts and sells to a muhrim, the sale is invalid but not void (fasid).
Valid vs. fasid: fasid means the contract is formed but deficient; batil means it never existed.
4
Analyze the rationale
The game is not mutaqawwim for the muhrim because he is prohibited from hunting and owning it. Hence his own transaction is void. But when the seller or buyer is non-muhrim, the property status changes.
The term ghayr mutaqawwim is key – the animal has no legal value for the muhrim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باع المحرم صيدا، أو اشتراه بطل. قوله: باع المحرم؛ أي حالة إحرامه صيداً اصطاده حالة إحرامه، فلو صاده محرماً وباعه حلالاً جاز البيع، ولو صاده حلالاً وباع محرماً فالبيع فاسد لا باطل. كذا في «السراج الوهاج». قوله: بطل؛ أي ذلك البيع والشراء؛ لأنّ الصيد في حقه غير متقوم ومحرّم.
A muhrim sells or buys game: it is void. Commentary: 'A muhrim sells' means while in his ihram, game that he hunted while in ihram. So if he hunted it as a muhrim and sold it to a non-muhrim, the sale is permissible; if he hunted it as a non-muhrim and sold it to a muhrim, the sale is invalid (fasid) not void. This is in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. 'It is void' means that sale and purchase are void because game is not considered property (ghayr mutaqawwim) and is prohibited to him.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage rules on transactions involving game for a muhrim. If a muhrim sells or buys game that was hunted while he was in ihram, the transaction is absolutely void (batil) because the animal is not considered valuable property for him. However, there are nuances: if a muhrim hunts the animal (while in ihram) and then sells it to a non-muhrim, that sale is valid because the buyer is not in ihram. Conversely, if a non-muhrim hunts the animal and sells it to a muhrim, the sale is fasid (invalid but not void) due to the muhrim's incapacity to own it. The commentary clarifies the conditions.

Key Terms
مُتَقَوِّم (mutaqawwim): Property that has legal value and can be traded; game for a muhrim is not mutaqawwim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a muhrim sells game that he hunted while in ihram to another muhrim?
A) The sale is valid.
B) The sale is void (batil).
C) The sale is invalid (fasid).
D) The sale is permissible if the price is paid.

Key Takeaway
A muhrim's transaction in game is void only if he hunted it himself; otherwise, it is either valid or fasid based on the parties' ihram status.

Blood Penalties for Qarin and Mufrid
Ruling on number of blood sacrifices for Qarin and Mufrid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the number of blood sacrifices owed by a Qarin and a Mufrid, and apply the exception when the pilgrim passes the miqat without ihram.

Core Idea
A Qarin normally owes two bloods—one for Hajj and one for Umrah—unless he passed the miqat without ihram, then only one.

In Hajj, certain violations (janayat) require expiatory blood sacrifices (dam). The Hanafi school distinguishes between the Mufrid (Hajj only) and the Qarin (combined Hajj and Umrah) in the number of bloods owed. This lesson unpacks that ruling, including a crucial exception when the pilgrim passes the miqat without assuming ihram.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rulings
State the number of blood sacrifices for a Mufrid and a Qarin as given in the text: Mufrid owes one blood, Qarin owes two bloods.
The Mufrid owes one blood if he passed the miqat without ihram; the text assumes that case.
2
Understand the exception
Note the exception: 'except if he passes the time (miqat) without being in ihram.' This reduces the Qarin's obligation to one blood.
The exception applies to the Qarin, not the Mufrid; the Mufrid still owes one blood.
3
Analyse the legal reasoning (talil)
The commentator explains that the obligation at the miqat is one ihram. Passing the miqat without ihram is a single violation (janaya), so only one blood is due.
This shows the Hanafi method of linking the ruling to the underlying cause (illah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على المفرد فعلى القارن دمان إلا بجواز الوقتِ غير محرم . والمراد بالوقت الميقات؛ لأنَّ الواجب عليه عند الميقات إحرام واحد. (ويثني جزاء صيد قتله محرمان، واتَّحد لو قتل صيد الحرم حلالان): فإنَّ ذلك جزاء الفعل، والفعل متعدد وجزاء صيد الحرم جزاء المحل، والمحل واحد.
On the Mufrid (one performing ifrad) then on the Qarin (one performing qiran) two bloods, except if he passes the time (miqat) without being in ihram. And what is meant by 'time' is the miqat; because what is obligatory upon him at the miqat is one ihram. (And double the penalty of a prey killed by two muhrims, and it is unified if two non-muhrims kill a prey of the Haram): for that is the penalty of the act, and the act is multiple, and the penalty of the Haram prey is the penalty of the non-muhrim, and the non-muhrim is one.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from the Hanafi school on blood penalties for a Qarin. The main text states that a Mufrid (one performing Hajj only) gives one blood sacrifice (dam), while a Qarin (one combining Hajj and Umrah) must give two bloods—one for Hajj and one for Umrah. However, there is an exception: if the Qarin passes the miqat (the appointed place for entering ihram) without having assumed ihram, and then later assumes ihram as a Qarin, he only owes one blood. This is because the obligation at the miqat is only one ihram; by passing it without ihram, he committed one violation (janaya), so only one blood is due. The commentator explains that the 'time' mentioned refers to the miqat. The passage then moves to a separate ruling: if two muhrims (persons in ihram) kill a single prey, each owes a separate penalty because the act of killing is attributed to each individually. In contrast, if two non-muhrims (hill) kill a prey within the Haram sanctuary, they owe only one penalty combined, because the penalty is for violating the sanctity of the Haram, and the non-muhrim is considered one legal person in this context. The reasoning is based on the nature of the penalty: it is for the act, and when the act is multiple (multiple killers), the penalty is multiple; when the act is single (the killing of haram prey by non-muhrims), the penalty is single.

Key Terms
المفرد (al-mufrid): One who performs Hajj only (ifrad) without combining Umrah.
القارن (al-qarin): One who performs Hajj and Umrah together (qiran) with a single ihram.
الميقات (al-miqat): The designated boundary where pilgrims must assume ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Two bloods
B) One blood
C) Three bloods
D) None

Key Takeaway
The number of blood sacrifices for a Qarin depends on whether he passed the miqat with or without ihram: normally two, but one if he passed without ihram.

Joint Killing Penalties for Muhrims and Non-Muhrims
Ruling on joint killing penalties for muhrims and non-muhrims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the penalty for prey killed by two muhrims versus two non-muhrims in the Haram.

Core Idea
When two muhrims kill one prey, each owes a full penalty; when two non-muhrims kill prey in the Haram, they share one penalty.

The same passage also addresses joint killing: if two muhrims (pilgrims in ihram) kill a single prey, each must pay a full penalty. In contrast, if two non-muhrims (people not in ihram) kill prey within the Haram sanctuary, they owe only one penalty combined. This lesson explains the reasoning behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Note the two cases: (1) two muhrims kill one prey, (2) two non-muhrims (hill) kill prey in the Haram.
The ruling is different for each.
2
State the penalty for each
For muhrims: the penalty is doubled (each owes a separate penalty). For non-muhrims: the penalty is united (they share one penalty).
The text says 'double the penalty' for muhrims, and 'it is unified' for non-muhrims.
3
Understand the legal reasoning
The commentator explains: the penalty is for the act (killing). When two muhrims kill, the act is attributed to each individually, so each owes a full penalty. When two non-muhrims kill in the Haram, the penalty is for violating the sanctity of the Haram, and the non-muhrims are considered one legal person (mahall) in this context.
This is an example of Hanafi talil based on the nature of the violation.

Penalty for Killing Prey: Multiple Acts vs Single Cause
Ruling on multiple penalties when two muhrims kill one prey

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on penalties when two muhrims kill one prey and explain the legal reasoning (talil) that distinguishes it from the case of two non-muhrims killing prey in the Haram.

Core Idea
When two muhrims kill one prey, each owes a penalty; when two non-muhrims kill in the Haram, only one penalty is due.

This passage from the Hanafi legal tradition clarifies a subtle distinction in the law of hunting penalties (jaza). While the prey is one, the number of penalties depends on whether the prohibition is attached to the act (ihram) or to the place (Haram). Understanding this talil is essential for applying the rulings correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Note the two cases: (1) two muhrims kill prey anywhere (since they are in ihram), and (2) two non-muhrims kill prey within the Haram.
2
State the ruling for each
For two muhrims: each owes a full penalty (jaza). For two non-muhrims in the Haram: only one penalty is due between them.
3
Trace the legal reasoning (talil)
The text explains: for muhrims, the penalty is for the act of killing (fi‘l), and since the act is multiple (each muhrim performed it), the penalty is multiple. For non-muhrims in the Haram, the penalty is for violating the sanctity of the place (mahall), and the place is one, so the penalty is one.
This is an example of ta‘lil bi al-illah: the effective cause (illa) differs in each case.
4
Compare with the opposing view
Imam al-Shafi‘i holds that the penalty is based on the prey itself, so one prey means one penalty divided among the killers, whether muhrim or not.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under the Hanafi ruling, two muhrims each offer a penalty (e.g., a sheep), while under the Shafi‘i ruling, they offer only one penalty jointly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُثنى جزاء صيد قتله محرمان، واتَّحد لو قتل صيد الحرم حلالان) : فإنَّ ذلك جزاء الفعل، والفعل متعدد وجزاء صيد الحرم جزاء المحل، والمحل واحد.
And double the penalty of a prey killed by two muhrims, and it is unified if two non-muhrims kill a prey of the Haram: for that is the penalty of the act, and the act is multiple, and the penalty of the Haram prey is the penalty of the non-muhrim, and the non-muhrim is one.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage provides the legal reasoning for a differential ruling on penalties for killing prey. When two muhrims (pilgrims in the state of ihram) together kill a single prey, each is obligated to give a full penalty (jaza). This is because the penalty is attached to the act of killing (fi'l), and since each of the two muhrims performed the act of killing, each incurs a separate liability. The fact that the killed prey is one does not reduce the obligation. In contrast, if two non-muhrims (hill, i.e., not in ihram) kill a prey within the sacred precincts of the Haram, they are jointly and severally liable for only one penalty. This is because the penalty for killing a Haram prey is a compensation for violating the sanctity of the Haram, which is considered a single legal cause (sabab) attached to the land, not to the persons. Thus, the non-muhrims are treated as a single entity with respect to the Haram's sanctity, and only one penalty is due. The commentator emphasizes that the distinction is based on the nature of the penalty: in the case of muhrims, the penalty is for the act of killing (which is multiple), while in the case of non-muhrims in the Haram, the penalty is for violating the Haram (which is one).

Key Terms
محرمان (muhriman): Two persons in the state of ihram.
حلالان (hillan): Two persons not in ihram (non-muhrims).
جزاء (jaza): The penalty or compensation for killing a prey while in ihram or within the Haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One penalty between both
B) Two penalties, one from each
C) Four penalties
D) No penalty because the prey is not within the Haram

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the number of penalties for killing prey depends on whether the prohibition is attached to the act (multiple killers = multiple penalties) or to the place (one penalty regardless of number).

Sale and Purchase of Prey by a Muhrim
Rulings on a muhrim's interaction with prey

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on a muhrim's sale, purchase, slaughter, and consumption of prey, and distinguish the liability of the slaughtering vs. non-slaughtering muhrim.

Core Idea
A muhrim's transaction in prey is void; slaughter makes meat haram; only the slaughterer pays compensation for eating.

This lesson examines the legal effects of a muhrim's interaction with hunted prey. The text presents a cascade of rulings: from the invalidity of sale/purchase to the consequences of slaughter and consumption. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for anyone performing Hajj or Umrah, as well as for those advising pilgrims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Note that the passage covers four scenarios: selling, buying, slaughtering, and eating prey. Determine the ruling (void, haram, or compensation) for each.
The overall context is the state of ihram.
2
Understand the basis for voidness
The sale and purchase are void (batil) because the prey is not considered property (mutaqawwim) for the muhrim; it is like wine or pork.
This is a strict Hanafi position.
3
Analyze the slaughter ruling
When a muhrim slaughters prey, the meat becomes haram for everyone because the slaughter is not legally recognized—akin to slaughtering a carcass.
The prohibition applies to all, not just the muhrim.
4
Distinguish liability for eating
If the slaughtering muhrim eats, he must pay the value of what he consumed. If another muhrim (who did not slaughter) eats, he has no financial liability; only istighfar is required.
The key difference is committing the act of slaughter.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Contrast with the Shafi'i view: a muhrim may sell prey if he hunted it before ihram. The Hanafi school rejects this entirely.
This affects fatwa for followers of different schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باع المحرم صيدا، أو شراهُ بطل . ولو ذبحه حرم، ولو أكل منه غرم قيمة ما أكل، لا محرم لم يذبحه
If a muhrim sells a prey, or buys it, the transaction is void. And if he slaughters it, it becomes unlawful (to eat), and if he eats from it, he incurs the value of what he ate, not (if) another muhrim who did not slaughter it.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings concerning a muhrim's interaction with hunted prey (sayd). The main text states: if a muhrim (a person in the state of ihram) sells a prey (that he hunted while in ihram), the sale is void (batal). Similarly, his purchase of such a prey is also void. The reason given in the commentary is that the prey is not considered legally valuable (ghayr mutaqawwim) for the muhrim; it is prohibited for him, like wine or pork. The transaction is akin to selling wine. Next, if the muhrim slaughters the prey (in a way that would normally make it halal), the meat becomes unlawful (haram) for everyone to eat because the slaughter by a muhrim is not legally recognized—it is like slaughtering a carcass. If the muhrim himself eats from that meat, he must pay the market value of what he consumed as a penalty for violating his ihram. However, if another muhrim (who did not slaughter it) eats from it, he is not liable for any payment; his only obligation is to seek forgiveness (istighfar) because he ate from what is considered dead meat (maytah) for him. The commentator explains that the liability of the slaughterer is due to his own ihram violation; the second muhrim is not penalized with a payment because he did not commit the act of slaughter.

Key Terms
بطل (batala): The transaction is void (batil) from the start, not merely defective (fasid).
حرم (haruma): The meat becomes unlawful (haram) to consume.
غرم (gharima): He must pay compensation (the market value of what he ate).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale is valid but makruh
B) The sale is void (batil)
C) The sale is fasid and can be corrected
D) The sale is valid if the buyer is not in ihram

Key Takeaway
A muhrim's sale or purchase of prey is void; if he slaughters, meat is haram; only the slaughterer pays for eating.

Eating Prey Slaughtered by a Muhrim
Ruling on liability when eating prey slaughtered by a muhrim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a muhrim who slaughters and eats prey, and distinguish the liability of the slaughterer from a non-slaughtering muhrim.

Core Idea
Slaughtering prey as a muhrim makes the meat haram and triggers compensation for the slaughterer who eats it; other muhrims need only repentance.

This lesson clarifies a specific scenario during hajj: a muhrim who slaughters prey (which is prohibited). The text explains the legal consequences for the slaughterer and for a different muhrim who may eat from that meat. Understanding this distinction is important for applying the rules of ihram violations correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a muhrim slaughtering prey while in ihram. The ruling: the meat becomes haram (unlawful) for consumption.
This is the baseline ruling from the text: 'ولو ذبحه حرم'.
2
Identify the consequence for the slaughterer who eats
If the slaughtering muhrim himself eats from that meat, he must pay compensation (ghurm) equal to the market value of what he ate.
The text says: 'ولو أكل منه غرم قيمة ما أكل' – payment is wajib for him.
3
Identify the consequence for another muhrim who eats
If a different muhrim (who did not slaughter) eats the meat, he does not pay any monetary compensation; his only obligation is to seek repentance (istighfar).
The text says: 'لا محرم لم يذبحه' – no payment for the non-slaughterer.
4
Understand the basis (talil)
The penalty is attached to the prohibited act of slaughter, not to the act of eating. The slaughterer committed the violation; the other muhrim only consumed carrion (maytah) for which istighfar suffices.
This distinction is explicit in the binary structure of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ذبحه حرم، ولو أكل منه غرم قيمة ما أكل، لا محرم لم يذبحه
And if he slaughters it, it becomes unlawful, and if he eats from it, he incurs the value of what he ate, not (if) another muhrim who did not slaughter it.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This is a continuation of the previous ruling. It specifies the consequences for a muhrim who slaughters a prey (which he hunted or possessed). After the invalid slaughter, the meat is considered haram (forbidden). If the same muhrim who performed the slaughter eats any part of that meat, he must pay the market value (ghurm) of the amount he consumed. This payment is a penalty for breaking the prohibition of hunting/consuming prey while in ihram. The phrase 'la muhrim lam yadbahhu' (not another muhrim who did not slaughter it) clarifies that if a different muhrim (who did not participate in the slaughter) eats from that meat, he is not obliged to pay any value. His only obligation is to seek repentance (istighfar) because for him the meat is considered carrion (maytah) since the slaughter was invalid. The liability of the slaughtering muhrim is due to his own act (violation of ihram), not mere consumption. This distinction is based on the principle that the penalty is tied to the prohibited act of hunting/slaughtering, not to eating as such.

Key Terms
غرم (gharima): To pay compensation equal to the market value of the consumed meat.
حرم (haruma): Declared unlawful (haram) for consumption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) He must seek forgiveness (istighfar) but no monetary payment
D) He must pay double the value

Key Takeaway
The ruling clarifies that only the muhrim who slaughters prey incurs a monetary penalty for eating it; other muhrims merely seek forgiveness.

Penalty for Female Gazelle from Haram and Her Offspring
Ruling on multiple penalties when a female gazelle taken out of Haram gives birth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on multiple penalties when a female gazelle taken from Haram gives birth after payment of the mother's penalty, and identify the legal cause (talil) for the difference.

Core Idea
After paying a game's penalty, the Haram protection (amn) ceases; offspring born thereafter incur no penalty.

This passage from the Hanafi legal tradition addresses a subtle question: if a female gazelle is removed from the Haram and later gives birth, does the person owe a separate penalty for each offspring? The answer hinges on whether the mother's penalty was paid before the birth. We will follow the reasoning (talil) that explains why the protection does not transfer after payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text presents two distinct cases: (1) the female gazelle is taken out of the Haram, gives birth, and both die; (2) the person first pays the penalty for the mother, then she gives birth, and both die.
2
Note the difference in outcome
In scenario 1, two penalties are due (one for mother, one for offspring). In scenario 2, only one penalty (for the mother) is due.
3
Understand the ruling for scenario 1
When both die and no penalty has been paid, the mother is still under the Haram protection (amn), and this protection extends to her offspring. Therefore, both are protected, and both require compensation.
4
Understand the ruling for scenario 2
If the mother's penalty is paid before the offspring is born, her protection ceases. Consequently, when the offspring is born after that, it does not inherit the protection, so no penalty is due for it. Only the mother's penalty was already discharged.
5
Extract the legal cause (talil)
The key factor is the timing of the penalty payment. The protection (amn) is a legal attribute that ceases upon discharge of the penalty. Offspring born after that cessation are not protected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولدت ظبية أخرجت من الحرم، وماتا غرمهما وإن أدى جزاءها، ثُمَّ وَلَدَت، لم يُجْزِه
A female gazelle that was taken out of the Haram gave birth, and both died, he incurs both penalties; and if he paid her penalty, then she gave birth, it does not suffice (i.e., the offspring's penalty is not needed).
Source pages p. 642

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where a person takes a female gazelle (or similar game) out of the Haram (the sacred sanctuary of Mecca). Upon removal, the gazelle is still under the protection (amn) of the Haram and must be returned. If, after removal, the gazelle gives birth and then both the mother and the newborn die, the person is liable for two penalties: one for the mother and one for the offspring. This is because both are considered protected (mustahiqq al-amn), and their death due to the person's action necessitates compensation. The penalty for each is like the penalty for killing a prey (e.g., a sheep or its equivalent). However, if the person first paid the penalty for the mother (i.e., discharged his liability for removing her) and then the mother gives birth and both die, he does not owe a penalty for the offspring. The commentary explains that after paying the mother's penalty, the mother is no longer considered protected (la tabqa amina). Consequently, the protection does not extend to the offspring, so no penalty is due for the offspring. This is because the protection (amn) is a legal attribute that ceases when the penalty is paid, and the offspring born after that does not inherit the protection.

Key Terms
ظبية (dhabiya): A female gazelle, used as an example of game (sayd) from the Haram.
أمن (amn): The legal protection/sanctity of the Haram area, making it unlawful to hunt or disturb.
جزاء (jaza): Penalty/compensation for violating the sanctity of the Haram by taking or killing the gazelle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A female gazelle taken from the Haram gives birth and both die. The person has not paid any penalty. How many penalties does the person owe?
A) One penalty (only mother)
B) Two penalties (mother and offspring)
C) No penalty
D) One penalty (only offspring)

Key Takeaway
The protection (amn) of the Haram ceases upon payment of the penalty for the mother, so offspring born after that incur no separate penalty.

Rulings on a Muhrim Slaughtering Game and Compensation for Game from the Haram
Explains rulings when a muhrim slaughters game and compensation for game taken from Haram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and apply the Hanafi rulings on a muhrim's liability for slaughtering game and for game taken from the Haram, including the transfer of compensation to offspring.

Core Idea
A muhrim who slaughters game renders it carrion and must compensate for what he eats; security status passes to offspring.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary clarifies two important scenarios for a person in ihram. First, if a muhrim slaughters game, the meat becomes like carrion, and he must pay the value of any portion he eats. Second, if a game animal is removed from the Haram and gives birth, the legal security status of the mother passes to the offspring, affecting compensation if they die. These rulings highlight the unique prohibitions and liabilities that apply within the sacred state and sanctuary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the muhrim did (slaughtering game) and the legal status of the meat (forbidden, like carrion).
The slaughter is legally invalid; the meat is not halal under any circumstance.
2
Distinguish liability for eating
Who is liable? The slaughterer must pay the value of what he ate; another muhrim who eats does not pay, only seeks forgiveness.
Liability stems from violating ihram prohibitions, not from eating per se.
3
Analyze the gazelle case
Consider the scenario: a gazelle taken from the Haram gives birth, then both die. The taker must pay compensation for both mother and offspring.
The security status of the mother passes to the offspring, making both subject to the obligation of return.
4
Determine the exception
If the taker already paid compensation for the mother before birth, then no compensation is due for the offspring.
Payment ends the security status of the mother, so it does not transfer to the unborn young.
5
Understand the underlying principle
The compensation obligation is based on the legal attribute of security (amn shar'i), which is akin to freedom or slavery in its transmissibility.
This analogy helps explain why the offspring inherits the status even if born outside the Haram.

And if he slaughters it, it becomes forbidden; and if he eats from it, he must pay the value of what he ate. (A female gazelle taken out of the sanctuary that gave birth, and both died: he must pay compensation for both): meaning compensation for the gazelle and the offspring. (And if he paid compensation for it, then it gave birth, that does not suffice): ... The eye, so it becomes like selling wine and swine and the like and buying them. (1) His statement: 'And if he slaughters it': meaning if the muhrim slaughters it in a legally valid manner, eating it is forbidden for everyone because his slaughter is not considered legally, so it becomes like slaughtering carrion or slaughtering by one who is not qualified for slaughter. [2] His statement: 'And if he eats from it...': meaning if the muhrim eats something from the game he slaughtered, he owes the value of the amount eaten. [3] His statement: 'Not a muhrim...': meaning if another muhrim eats something from the game slaughtered by a muhrim, the eater does not owe its value; because for eating carrion, nothing is required except seeking forgiveness, while the muhrim slaughterer owes it due to it being a prohibition of his ihram. [4] His statement: 'A female gazelle gave birth...': meaning if he takes out a gazelle or similar from the sanctuary, and it gives birth afterwards, and both die, he must pay compensation for each of them. [0] His statement: 'he must pay compensation for both': because the game after being taken out of the sanctuary remains entitled to legal security, because of which it must be sent back and returned, and entitlement to security is a legal attribute like slavery and freedom, so it passes to the offspring, even if its existence is in the outside, so the command to return continues or encompasses both. So if he seizes them and they die, the cause of liability is established for all. Unlike if he paid compensation for the gazelle after taking it out, and it gave birth afterwards, then the compensation for the offspring is not obligatory; because the gazelle after paying compensation no longer remains secure nor entitled to return, so that attribute does not exist in its offspring, such that compensation becomes obligatory due to its loss. (1) Meaning: he does not owe compensation for the offspring because after paying compensation for the mother, she no longer remains secure, and due to the non-transmission of security to the offspring then. See: Durar al-Hukkam (1:254) and al-Durr al-Muntaqa (1:302).
Source pages p. 642

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn that states multiple rulings related to a muhrim (person in ihram) and game. The rulings are: (1) If a muhrim slaughters game, the meat is forbidden for all, because the slaughter is legally invalid—like slaughtering a carcass or slaughter by an unqualified person. (2) If he eats from it, he must pay the value of what he ate as compensation. (3) However, if another muhrim eats from that same animal, that other muhrim is not liable for compensation; he only seeks forgiveness for eating carrion. The liability is specific to the slaughterer because he violated the restrictions of ihram. (4) Regarding a gazelle taken out of the Haram (sanctuary) that gives birth: if both the mother and offspring die, the person who took them out must pay compensation for both. This is because the legal status of 'security' (amn shar'i) attaches to the animal and passes to its offspring, making them all subject to the obligation of return. But if he had already paid compensation for the mother before the birth, then the offspring is not subject to compensation because the mother's security-satus ended upon payment. The commentary uses qiyas (analogy) to carrion and to selling wine and pigs to explain the invalidity of the slaughter.

Key Terms
محرم (muhrim): A person in the state of ihram (ritual consecration for Hajj or Umrah).
صيد (sayd): Game animal that is hunted.
جزاء (jaza'): Compensation paid for killing or causing loss of game while in ihram or within the Haram.
أمن شرعي (amn shar'iy): The legal status of inviolability that protects certain animals from being harmed within the Haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The meat is forbidden and he must pay the value of what he ate.
C) The meat is forbidden but no payment is required.
D) The meat is halal if he intended to eat it.

Key Takeaway
The muhrim's slaughter is invalid, making the meat carrion; the slaughterer must compensate for consumption, and game from the Haram retains security status that passes to offspring unless already compensated.

The Obligation of Ihram at the Miqat: Intention or Entry?
Explaining the ruling for a non-Makkan crossing the miqat without ihram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether intending Hajj/Umrah is a condition for the obligation of ihram at the miqat, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Crossing the miqat to enter Mecca makes ihram wajib — intention of Hajj or Umrah is incidental.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a critical nuance in Hanafi fiqh: does the obligation to assume ihram at the miqat depend on one's intention? The matn seems to condition it on intending Hajj or Umrah, but the commentator clarifies that the relied-upon school position is unconditional. Understanding this refinement prevents a common misunderstanding and ensures correct practice for all entering Mecca.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent ruling in the matn
Read the matn: it states that an afaqi 'intending Hajj or Umrah' who crosses the miqat and then assumes ihram owes a dam. Identify the condition mentioned: intending Hajj/Umrah.
This is the surface reading.
2
Recognize the commentator's caution
The commentator points out that 'intending Hajj or Umrah' could be misunderstood. He states that the correct Hanafi position is that anyone passing the miqat to enter Mecca must assume ihram, regardless of intention.
The condition is metaphorical, not literal.
3
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Consider a person who crosses the miqat for trade or tourism. According to the apparent reading, no ihram is needed; according to the mufta bihi, ihram is wajib and a dam is due if omitted.
This is the real-world impact of the dispute.
4
Locate the tarjih authority
The commentary cites Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir and al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad as authorities for the unconditional view. Note that later scholars (al-Haskafi, Ibn Abidin) confirm this.
This establishes the mufta bihi.
5
Apply the correct ruling
For any person (afaqi or returning Makkan) crossing the miqat to enter Mecca, the ruling is: ihram is wajib. If omitted, a dam becomes due. Intention of Hajj/Umrah is not a separate condition.
The act of entering Mecca itself triggers the obligation.

Chapter on Crossing the Time Without Ihram. An afaqi intending Hajj or Umrah crosses his time (miqat), then assumes ihram: a dam is incumbent on him. If he returns and assumes ihram... [Commentary]: (An afaqi intending Hajj or Umrah) crosses his time: i.e., his miqat. (Then assumes ihram: a dam is incumbent on him, and if he returns and assumes ihram): i.e., he only said 'intending Hajj or Umrah' so that if he did not intend either, nothing is incumbent on him due to crossing the miqat. [1] His statement: 'Afaqi': its meaning is everyone who is not from the people of Mecca, nor from the people of what is between them and the miqat, and it is more general, whether in reality or legally. For if a Makkan travels to other lands and then returns to Mecca and passes the miqat, ihram is also incumbent on him, and leaving it necessitates a dam. (2) His statement: 'intending Hajj or Umrah': its apparent meaning is that if he did not intend them, but rather tourism or trade, ihram is not incumbent on him, nor (does leaving it necessitate) a dam, and this is against the madhhab. For the books of the madhhab state clearly the obligation of ihram on the one passing the miqat, and the obligation of a dam for leaving it, even if he did not intend either of the two rites, as verified in Fath al-Qadir. And we have elaborated on its pros and cons in al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad 'ala Muwatta' Muhammad. So this condition must be considered incidental, and it is said that it is a metaphor for intending to enter Mecca. [3] His statement: 'if he returns': i.e., to any miqat, whether that miqat which he passed or another miqat; its recompense will come later. [4] His statement: 'due to crossing' is connected to his statement 'nothing is incumbent'; i.e., due to the crossing. Durar: (1) What the sharh issued and Ibn Kamal Pasha followed him in it... Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on the ruling for a person from outside the miqat (afaqi) who crosses the miqat without assuming ihram. The matn states that an afaqi who intends Hajj or Umrah and passes his miqat without ihram, then assumes ihram later, must offer a sacrificial animal (dam). The commentator then clarifies that the condition 'intending Hajj or Umrah' might be misunderstood; the relied-upon Hanafi position is that anyone who passes the miqat with the intention of entering Mecca must assume ihram, regardless of whether they specifically intend the rites. This is because the phrase is interpreted as a metaphor for intending to enter Mecca. The commentary cites Fath al-Qadir and other authorities to affirm that the correct view is that the obligation is attached to the act of entering Mecca, not the specific intention of Hajj/Umrah. The note adds that this is a common misunderstanding that some later scholars corrected.

Key Terms
آفاقي (afaqi): A person who is not a resident of Mecca nor of the area between Mecca and the miqat; effectively anyone from beyond the miqat.
ميقات (miqat): The designated boundary line beyond which a person intending to enter Mecca must assume ihram.
دم (dam): A sacrificial animal (usually a sheep) required as compensation for omitting a wajib of Hajj or Umrah.
إحرام (ihram): The state of ritual consecration for Hajj or Umrah, which includes wearing two unstitched garments and abstaining from certain acts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) They must offer a sacrifice even if they assume ihram.
D) They must return to the miqat and assume ihram there.

Key Takeaway
The obligation to assume ihram at the miqat is triggered by the act of entering Mecca, not by the specific intention of Hajj or Umrah.

Ruling for Passing the Miqat Without Ihram and the Effect of Returning
Commentary on rulings for passing miqat without ihram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on passing the miqat without ihram, explain the effect of returning to the miqat, and evaluate Zufar's objection.

Core Idea
Passing the miqat without ihram incurs a dam; returning to the miqat before rites drops it.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies the ruling on a person who passes the miqat without assuming ihram. It addresses key terms, corrects a common misunderstanding about intention, and discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute between the majority and Zufar on whether returning to the miqat after having assumed ihram elsewhere cancels the dam. You will learn the rule, its proof (istihsan), and the practical consequence of the difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and base ruling
An Aafaqi (non-Meccan) who intends Hajj or Umrah, passes the miqat without ihram, then later assumes ihram: a dam (sacrificial animal) is wajib upon him.
2
Clarify the condition 'intends Hajj or Umrah'
The commentator corrects that the madhhab actually requires ihram for anyone passing the miqat, regardless of intention; the condition in the matn is figurative (kinaayah) for intending to enter Mecca.
Key: A person passing the miqat for trade or tourism also must assume ihram and owes a dam if omitted.
3
Understand the effect of returning to the miqat
If the person returns to any miqat (before starting Hajj/Umrah actions) and assumes ihram there, the dam is dropped by consensus of the Hanafi imams (except Zufar).
The return must be as a muhrim (already in ihram) and before beginning the rites (e.g., before talbiyah).
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute with Zufar
Zufar argues that the dam, being a penalty for the original violation (passing without ihram), is not expunged by returning; the majority (Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn) hold it drops because the missed obligation was made up in its proper time, based on istihsan.
Proof: istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas (strict analogy).
5
Apply the ruling
Given a scenario, determine whether a dam is owed or dropped, checking the conditions: must be an Aafaqi (or returning Meccan), passing miqat without ihram, returning before rites and reciting talbiyah to drop the dam.
If he returns after starting rites, the dam remains.

[1] قوله : آفاقي ؛ المراد به كلُّ مَن ليس من أهل مكة، ولا من أهل ما بينهما وبين الميقات، وهو أعم من أن يكون حقيقة أو حكماً ، فإنّ المكي إذا سافر إلى بلادِ أُخر، ثمّ
رجع إلى مكة ومرّ على الميقات يلزمُهُ الإحرام أيضاً، ويجب بتركه الدم. [٢) قوله : يريد الحج والعمرة ؛ ظاهره أنه لو لم يقصدهما، بل السياحة أو التجارة لا يلزمه الإحرام، ولا بتركه الدم، وهذا خلافُ المذهب، فإنّ كتب المذهب ناطقة بلزوم الإحرام على المار بالميقات، ولزوم الدم بتركه وإن لم يقصد أحد النسكين
كما حققه في «فتح القدير». وبسطنا ما له وما عليه في «التعليق الممجد على موطأ محمد)، فلا بد أن يجعل هذا القيد اتفاقياً، ويقال: إنّه كناية عن إرادة دخول مكة. [۳] قوله : فإن عاد ؛ أي إلى ميقات ما ، سواء كان ذلك الميقات الذي جاوز عنه أو میقات آخر، وسيأتي جزاؤه. [٤]قوله : بمجاوزة ؛ متعلّق بقوله : «لا يجب» ؛ أي بسبب التجاوز.

أو محرماً لم يشرع في نُسُك ولبى سقط دَمُه ، وإلا فلا

Chapter of passing the time without ihram: An Aafaqi who intends Hajj or Umrah passes its time (i.e., its miqat), then assumes ihram, a dam is incumbent on him. If he returns and assumes ihram... Commentary: [1] Chapter of passing the time without ihram: 'Aafaqi who intends Hajj or Umrah' passes its time: meaning his miqat, then assumes ihram, a dam is incumbent on him; if he returns and assumes ihram: meaning he only said 'intends Hajj or Umrah' so that if he does not intend anything from them, nothing is obligatory on him due to passing the miqat. [1] His saying: Aafaqi; what is meant is everyone not from the people of Mecca nor from the people of what is between them and the miqat, whether literally or legally, for a Meccan if he travels to other lands then returns to Mecca and passes by the miqat, ihram is also incumbent upon him, and by omitting it a dam is obligatory. [2] His saying: intends Hajj and Umrah; its apparent meaning is that if he does not intend them, but rather tourism or trade, ihram is not necessary for him, nor a dam for omitting it, and this is contrary to the madhhab, for the books of the madhhab explicitly state the obligation of ihram upon the one passing the miqat and the obligation of a dam for omitting it even if he does not intend either of the two rites, as verified in 'Fath al-Qadir'. And we have elaborated on its pros and cons in 'al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad 'ala Muwatta' Muhammad'. So this condition must be considered accidental, and it is said that it is a metaphor for intending to enter Mecca. [3] His saying: if he returns; meaning to any miqat, whether that miqat which he passed or another miqat, and its consequence will come. [4] His saying: due to passing; relates to his saying 'not obligatory', i.e., because of the passing. ... [PAGE_644] 'or while in ihram having not begun the rites and he performed talbiyah, his dam is dropped, otherwise not.' 'Umdat al-Ri'aya 'ala Sharh al-Wiqaya - [1] And his saying: then he assumed ihram; there is no need for this condition, for if he does not assume ihram, the dam is also obligatory upon him. So the correct expression would be to say: he passed its time, a dam is incumbent upon him. And it can be answered: He only mentioned his saying 'then he assumed ihram' to let it be known that the dam is not dropped by this ihram, contrary to the case where he returns to the miqat and assumes ihram, for then the dam drops because he made up for the right of the miqat. Then his saying: if he returns and assumes ihram; its meaning: if he did not assume ihram from the miqat, then returned to the miqat and assumed ihram, then the dam drops by agreement. ... (Or while in ihram) having not begun the rites and performed talbiyah, his dam drops, otherwise not: meaning if he assumed ihram after passing, then returned to the miqat before beginning the rites while reciting talbiyah, the dam drops according to us, contrary to Zufar, for according to him the dam does not drop. [A] [1] His saying: if he does not assume ihram; i.e., after passing, rather he entered Mecca likewise. [2] His saying: the correct expression; i.e., what would be correct and appropriate is to delete this misleading condition, because of the non-obligation of dam when not assuming ihram. [3] His saying: about him; i.e., about the objection raised on mentioning the condition. (4) His saying: by agreement; i.e., among our imams; because he made up for what he missed in its time, so only sin remains due to the passing. [0] His saying: or muhrim; i.e., he returned to the miqat while being a muhrim not having begun any of the actions of Hajj. [6] His saying: not having begun; a circumstantial clause after a circumstantial clause, so they are two overlapping or synonymous circumstantial clauses. [7] His saying: otherwise not; if he returns to it after beginning the rites or did not perform talbiyah in it, it does not drop. [8] His saying: for according to him the dam does not drop; because the obligation of the dam is due to the offense which is his passing the miqat without ihram, and it is not removed by returning to it, so there is no basis for dropping what is obligated by it. And our proof: he made up for the omitted act in its time, and that is before beginning the actions, so the dam drops, contrary to the case where he assumed ihram after passing and began the rites then returned to it and performed talbiyah, for then he did not make up for what (he omitted).
Source pages p. 643

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on the chapter of passing the miqat without ihram. It opens by stating the ruling: an Aafaqi (non-Meccan) who intends Hajj or Umrah, passes the miqat without ihram, then later assumes ihram, must slaughter a dam (sacrifice). If he returns to a miqat (any miqat) and assumes ihram there, the dam is dropped. The commentator then clarifies each term. 'Aafaqi' includes all non-Meccans, even a Meccan returning from travel who passes a miqat. Importantly, the commentator corrects a potential misunderstanding from the condition 'intends Hajj or Umrah'. The apparent meaning is that if one passes the miqat without intending pilgrimage (e.g., for trade), no dam is due. However, the true Hanafi madhhab is that passing the miqat even without such intention obligates ihram and a dam for omitting it. The condition is thus figurative (kinaayah) for intending to enter Mecca. The commentator cites Fath al-Qadir and his own work on Muwatta' Muhammad. He also discusses the phrase 'then he assumed ihram'—pointing out that even without assuming ihram, a dam is due. The reason for mentioning it is to show that this later ihram does not drop the already incurred dam, unlike returning to the miqat and assuming ihram there, which does drop it by consensus of the Hanafi imams. Then the passage discusses a specific scenario: if one passes the miqat, assumes ihram later, and then returns to the miqat while already in ihram but before beginning any Hajj/Umrah actions (i.e., before reciting talbiyah), and then recites talbiyah at the miqat, the dam is dropped according to the Hanafis (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad). This is contrary to Zufar's opinion, who argues that the dam was caused by the original violation (passing without ihram) and returning does not undo it. The Hanafi response is that the pilgrim has made up for the missed obligation within its time (before starting acts), so the dam falls. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference).

Key Terms
آفاقي (āfāqī): A person not from the city of Mecca nor from the area between Mecca and the miqat (the boundaries for Hajj).
ميقات (mīqāt): The designated time or place for entering ihram for Hajj/Umrah.
دم (dam): A sacrificial animal (sheep or goat) required as a penalty for certain violations during Hajj/Umrah.
مُحرِم (muḥrim): One who has entered the state of ihram for Hajj or Umrah.
تلبية (talbiyah): The recitation 'Labbayk Allahumma labbayk...' as part of entering ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the ruling for an Aafaqi who passes the miqat without ihram intending trade, and then later assumes ihram?
A) No dam is required because he did not intend Hajj or Umrah.
B) A dam is required because passing the miqat without ihram obligates it.
C) He must return to the miqat and assume ihram again to avoid dam.
D) He owes two dams: one for passing and one for assuming ihram late.

Key Takeaway
Passing the miqat without ihram incurs a dam; returning to the miqat as a muhrim before starting rites and reciting talbiyah drops the dam per the majority Hanafi view.

The Obligation of Talbiyah When Returning to the Miqat to Drop Dam
Commentary on miqat, ihram, and dam with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on passing the miqat without ihram, explain the condition for dropping the dam when returning, and distinguish Abu Hanifa's view from the Sahibayn's on the requirement of talbiyah.

Core Idea
Passing the miqat without ihram incurs a wajib dam; returning as a muhrim alone does not drop it—Abu Hanifa insists on both ihram and talbiyah.

Imagine a pilgrim from Mecca intends to perform Hajj but forgets to assume ihram before passing the miqat. He realizes his mistake and returns to the miqat. Is simply entering ihram enough to waive the sacrificial penalty? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on whether the talbiyah must also be recited. This lesson examines the proof and practical consequence of this dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core obligation
What is required of any pilgrim when they reach the miqat? Determine the two elements that become obligatory (wajib) upon reaching the miqat as a non-muhrim.
The text states: 'when he reached the miqat as a non-muhrim, talbiyah and ihram became obligatory upon him at the miqat.'
2
Contrast the two Hanafi views
Compare Abu Hanifa's position (requires both ihram and talbiyah upon return) with the Sahibayn's (only ihram is needed). Note the wording: the matn says 'walabba' to exclude the Sahibayn's view.
3
Trace the rational proof (dalil aqli)
Understand the istihsan (juristic preference) behind Abu Hanifa's view: since both ihram and talbiyah were missed at the miqat, both must be performed upon return to fulfill the obligation.
The commentary on 'kaaf' explains the logic.
4
Apply the mufta bihi position
Determine the operative ruling for fatwa: based on the matn's inclusion of 'walabba', the relied-upon view is that talbiyah is required.
Tarjih authority: 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah.

(كمكي يريد الحج ، ومتمتع فرغ من عمرته، وخرجا من الحرم وأحرما : تشبيه بالمسألة المتقدّمة في لزومِ الدَّم، فإنَّ إحرام المكي من الحرم، والمتمتع بالعمرة لَمَّا دخل مكة، وأتى بالعمرة صارَ مكَّيَّاً ، وإحرامه من الحرم ، فيجب عليهما دم لمجاوزة الميقات ، بلا إحرام.
(فإن دَخَلَ كوفي (۳) البُستان لحاجة (٤)
فات في أوانه ، فلا يسقط الدم عنه.
(۱) قوله : كاف؛ لأن الواجب عليه هو أن يكون محرماً عند الميقات لا أن ينشئ الإحرام عنده ألا ترى أنه لو أحرم قبل أن ينتهي إلى الميقات، ثم مر بالميقات محرماً ولم يلبيا عند الميقات لا يلزمه شيء. وعنده يعود ملبياً محرماً ، لما أنّه لما انتهى إلى الميقاتِ حلالاً وجبت عليه التلبية عند الميقات والإحرام، فإذا ترك ذلك بالمجاوزة حتى أحرم وراء الميقات ثم عاد، فإن لبى أتى بجميع ما هو المستحق عليه، فيسقط عنه ، وإن لم يلب لم يأتِ بما استحق عليه ؛ فلذا لا يسقط عنه الدم ما لم يلب. كذا في «النهاية».
